RATING: 10
121231
I do not like suicide Tichu anymore.  Not after learning this nifty passing convention.  I will not play Tichu without this convention any more.  Its a little convoluted but here it is.  If you draw one of the power6 cards (dragon, phoenix or aces) then pass it to your partner. If you draw two, keep them both and pass an even card (or Q) to your partner.  If you draw none, then pass an odd card (or J,K).   Makes for a great game!

111021
This is a game if I ever play in a group regularly that will become a classic.  A little getting used to if not used to climbing card games.  Otherwise, not that hard, but the partnership aspect really makes it a totally different beast.  A hell of a lot more stressful than 13.  Still its great,but needs a different mindset.  Several plays later, I still find the allowable sets annoying.  I like it cause everyone knows it, but I'd prefer to try some other climbing games before settling on this one.

110100
I really, really liked playing Suicide Tichu.  I might drop it if I like my Tichu/VC version more, but until I get it played, I'll take Suicide Tichu any day.  (Suicide Tichu, every hand one partner on each team must call Tichu or grand).
  
100100
But now I have decided that what I really, really, really like is playing mega agressive tichu with no care for ultimate winning.  Unfortunately this requires a partner who is willing to go along, but when it does, its a lovely game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very smart team cardgame (2vs2). Involves a fair amount of luck, but skill is also important to increase win rates, up to a point of 65%-70%, if playing with average players. There is great amount of strategy involved to increase your chance to win. It is extremely addictive and can cause great havoc among teammates, when someone does something not so bright, or gets very unlucky!
A set up to 1000 points, can take from 1 hour up to 4 hours since there are also negative points so be wary!

Sure, BGGers tend to be hostile to mainstream games with a million analysts, like Bridge or Poker, but there's a lot of great deep but relatively unanalysed games out there like Solo Whist, Scopone or Piquet, and I just don't see how it is that Tichu rates the pants off them.

RATING: 4
I played Asshole nearly every day at lunch in high school and this is much better. The partnership aspect makes the game what it is more so than anything else. I kind of wish the bombs were played in turn order, rather than how they are.

This might be a 9 but I'm a little burned out on it after only 12 plays just because when we play it it's all we play and we tend to play this rather than other games I want to play, which sucks.

Update: playing the android version (while sometimes boring due to the lack on human element) has taught me a lot about aggressive calling but has diminished my opinion of the game somewhat due to the predictability element. 

another update: I continue to grow bored with this game. It's good, but the honeymoon period is over in a big way.

yet another update: After playing some trick taking games (notably 42 and Tarock games) I'm pretty sure I prefer them. I also don't know why a blown call accrues negative points instead of adding to your opponents' score. The way the rules are written the game could theoretically never end. 

After continuing to discover and play more trick-takers I don't know why anyone plays climbing games. There's no discovery phase and watching someone go out because nobody can play on a full house is utterly boring. I don't despise Tichu but if I never played it again I wouldn't care.

RATING: 7
Simple game with a lot of depth when you get into it. Quick rounds helps offset bad rounds with good rounds though annoying luck fluctuations can lead to runaway winners. It's giant flaw is the "Calling Tichu" mechanic. Rather than having a game mechanic which would assist what would be considered a "bad hand" the Tichu bet takes a hand that would have already been considered a good hand without the bet and turns it into a fantastic hand.

RATING: 8
4

It's pretty addictive. I've played "Big Two" growing up, and the extra cards are great for adding strategy to the game and passing adds to the strategy. Obviously, there will be hands that are ridiculously strong and some that are total garbage. It's nice, though, that long straights are really nice to have but, often, having one will mean that the rest of your hand is going to be terrible and you may have difficulty getting the lead. Likewise, having lots of control cards is really good but, while you'll often be able to end up with the lead, it takes a lot of turns to go out and losing the lead just once may let a mega-straight player go out ahead of you. And etc.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
An interesting game with unusual mechanics.  A four-player partnership climbing game. Tichu presents lots of decisions; the most subtle around when to break up a set to take a trick and get the lead, and whether to play for yourself or to help your partner.  How best to use the four special cards remains a conundrum for me.  It's clever that the phoenix and dragon are both extremely useful and present something of a problem.  The more I play, the more I enjoy Tichu.

RATING: 7.5
I have to admit, I am just a sucker for any trick-taking card game.  At first play, I welcomed Tichu with a gooey heart.  And I loved its pokers style multi-card-trick-taking, mixing many classic card game styles.  It all works rather well surprisingly, however that is not to say it doesn't get a little messy in some places.  For one, I have trouble with the calling "Tichu" bit, it changes the basic mechanic of trying to get points anymore, and turns the game into an "uno" style rusher.  For me this a jarringly rapid change of thought.  Also Tichu is largely based on luck, with good hands dominating, compared to other more balanced card games like Bridge, or Gin.  What Tichu does though is take some elements from the old guys like (a lot of Bridge, and poker), and tries hard to entertain all at once.  It most certainly does, but don't expect it to kill off its predecessors.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This and Fairy Tale are both fantastic four player partnership card games.  I like that light cooperative-competitive genre a lot, and I find it is worth owning both of them for variety.

Tichu is much like a trick taking game on the surface, but with a lot of important differences.  

The overall structure is like other games.  You get your cards.  You pass a card to your opponents and your partner.  If you think you have the best hand you call Tichu which means you get extra points for going out first but lose points if you don't make it.  Then everyone those cards in and the final best play takes the pot and any points in it.

The difference it that instead of following card suits you play a set similar to poker hands -- single card, full house, three of kind, etc.  If anyone else wants to play they have to follow that set -- but you can always pass.

And while some cards have point values, the real points come from going out as quickly as possible.

So the fun is analyzing your hand and figuring out how to group up your cards to get rid of them all.  And if your hand is really good, trying to figure out ahead of time if you will be able to go out first.

Added to all this are special cards with special powers as well as the trump-like Bombs which can unexpectedly steal a hand.

RATING: 8.7
Tichu is awesome - it may well be the most interesting of all the climbing games.  On the negative it sits on the fence between lifestyle and casual game and unless you have a regular group who all love it you might not get to play it that much.  It needs people who like partnership games, can be forgiving, can cope with frustrations and enjoy the tense excitement that Tichu can bring.  They also need to enjoy the subtleties of the scoring system which is a mix of bets, trying to get key tricks, trying to get a 1-2 (you and your partner out before either opponent) and avoiding going out last (tricks dont score).  After poker and bridge this might be my favorite standard card game but need more plays to know for sure.  Its not the most versatile its 4 player only and whilst you could introduce new gamers to it I might try people on clubs, haggis or Franks Zoo first. I do miss the equalising element of everyone starting with 3 jokers/bomb cards in Haggis and the looping structure of Franks Zoo (aces are very awesome in this game as are the special cards).  There is more than enough to make up for this though although you need to be very patient as there will be times where you and your partner are not on the same wavelength and you mess up hands.

Second Play: I can only endorce my previous sentiments and reduce the rating from 5.5 to 4. This is just a twist on a trick taking card game. There is nothing here to make it any different from a standard deck of cards. This does not warrant its own slot on BGG. What is all the fuss about?

RATING: 4.5
I can see why certain people will rate this very high, but I would rather track down some of those CIA camps here in Europe and volunteer for a few hours of white noise than listen to a quick explanation of what is a sensible way to play your hand.

It says a lot about my familiarity with trick-taking partnership games that when people talk about "conventions" for a game I am genuinely surprised that what at first glance appears to be a normal card game with its own set of arbitrary quirky rules, can apparently support its own pro-league and fan gatherings.    

It's not true that I have an inbuilt dislike of card games, or have a special beef with trick taking card games. "Lost Cities" is actually one of the highest ranking games on my list, and having discovered partnership play for that one has opened up a whole new world still. I love 99. When "Mu & Mehr" was first explained to me I completely glazed over, but that certainly had quite a few elements that made me want to learn the game. In my teens I frequently played a Bridge-light variation that I still instinctively prefer over its Big Mama. And "Zoff im Zoo" is another trick-taking one that I play with joy.

This, on the other hand, typifies the very sort of trick-taking card game I strongly dislike. Too many rules that seem to have no inner logic ("a mosquito sting enrages an elephant and makes it count for 2" is the sort of narrative I appreciate), too many arbitrary combos that are or are not allowed (why can I play 2 or 4 but not 3 of the same kind?), a lucky opening hand can set someone up for a gazillion points and there is often sod all you or your partner or even you and your partner can do about it. And why are certain things worth 100 points? Why not 150? And  then there are bombs, or trumps, or parakiets, or whatever, that you have or haven't got and can be another random lucky way to ruin thought out and decent gameplay. For some reason that bothers me a lot more in card games than in boardgames.

At least it left the suit-order intact, otherwise I would have signed up for a whole fortnight at Uncle Sam's pleasure.

I like the simplicity of Zoff im Zoo, and the way some normally weak cards can trump them for obvious reasons. I am still blown away by what Lost Cities has to offer for the few variables it deals with, probably surpassed only by "Geschenkt". I like the gentle incremental bidding in Mu&Mehr, where people slowly reveal information that offer you a whole range of options and how they play out.

To me, decisions about contracts (see, I'm learning card-speak), opening cards, information revealed to partners etc should be a slow and gentle dance with frequent dilemmas. Here someone declares Tichu and whacks three quarter of their hand on the table (two of which were lucky hand-me-downs) and then quickly afterwards get a gazillion points. Big deal.

I probably will play it more often as it is a highly liked game by some in our group, probably discover it is more of a gambling game with small pockets of information than a card game (it has to be if it comes from China), and might even get a bit better at it, gaining a small degree of control. But the moments where clever card play and anticipated dilemmas will keep me thrilled seem for now too thin on the ground. And when I can instantly think of a handful of other, less fiddly and random-rule heavy games that offer that same excitement far more frequently without relying on a lot of chrome to throw in the wackiness, I will have to persuade myself hard not to moan too loud when it gets suggested.

End of rant.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

RATING: 7.5
I'm told that this game is similar to a game called A$$hole, and I remember it being similar to The Great Dalmuti.  Tichu is a card game that centers around taking 'tricks', and getting points over many hands.

As a card game, it has a high degree of luck involved, but since it is played over many hands, the luck tends to even out, and the more skilled players will tend to do better.

This is a fun little game.  It is good natured and you can have a conversation while playing it.  Good for an afternoon or evening with an even number of friends.

Solo he jugado un par de rondas en plan tutorial para sentir el funcionamiento del juego. RATING: 5
My first play was particularly bad, both in the cards drawn and (of course) by my poor play.  I'll freely admit that I am horrible at climbing games and this results in a small hatred of them.  Gang of Four fares no better. 

A second play left a more favourable impression.  Closer score, and though it's still hard to see what to do, I had a lot of fun screwing up.

To me, an ideal game is one that, when you win, you feel like you're the better player, but when you lose you blame your bad luck.  Tichu is the only game I can think of that's the opposite.  You have enough control that when you lose you feel like you screwed up, yet when you win, you're not sure why you did and it could just be the luck of the cards.  That can result in a frustrating experience.

I can see what people here like about the game, since they love stressful decisions and turn angst.  I don't like those things, so I'm not sure whether I actually [i]like[/i] the game, but for the most part I'm OK to play if other people want to.  There are many other games I'd choose to play instead, though, so...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I always loved partnership card games and this one has quickly become my favorite. It hits the perfect spot, with relatively simple rules and lots of decisions and possibilities to help your partner.

The passing of a card to your teammate adds a nice signaling opportunity. There are different ways to earn points: finishing first (with or without Tichu announced) and points from tricks. Playing until 1000 points will put pressure on the team that is behind and force them to take more risks, that is, announce Tichu and even Grand Tichu more often.

There are of course stronger hands and weaker hands, but with the many possibilities of playing card combinations and especially because of bombs, practically no hand is a guaranteed win. And no hand is so weak that you cannot influence the game with it, you can always help your partner or at least try to prevent the other team from going first and second.

RATING: 4
Who knew that a font existed which could render a standard French set of playing cards near illegible? I've been playing with the numbers one to ten with the court cards all my life, yet somehow the makers of Tichu have chosen a font so hideous that I can have trouble identifying the cards and have an unnatural urge to look away from my cards while playing. Basically the least useable set of cards I have ever encountered.

As to the game itself, it's a little too long what with all of the yo yo ing points, and rounds are only really interesting if a tichu is called. Which is a problem because you can't actually call tichu until you've played the game a number of times in order to be able to identify what a good hand is. You might notice that what I've just described is a huge barrier to entry. The game isn't fun unless you have lots of experience. But to get to that experience you'll have to slog through your least fun games to get there. I already own plenty of fun games for four that are fun right away, there's no need for me to play this one.

RATING: 10
I never tire of Tichu. No matter how much I play. Every game of Tichu is exciting and interesting. There's an impressive amount of depth to Tichu - I've played countless games and still don't feel like I've mastered it. Knowing when to call Tichu and how to break up your hand can be very tense, and because there's so many ways to split up your cards while trying to go out fast, take tricks with lots of points, AND support your partner, Tichu gives you a lot to think about. Luck of the draw factors in, of course, but after playing and witnessing countless games I can confidently say that the experienced players always do better. A perfect game.

Update: Our group has been playing this for some 8 years now and it STILL gets play at every single weekly gamenight we have. For eight. Years. If I hadn't rated this a 10 right off the damn bat it certainly would have one now!

RATING: 7.4
I gave this game a hard time in the past.  I think because I didn't and still don't think it as good a game as say Pinochle, Bridge and Spades.  However, this game is much more fun to play as those games.  To make it so, you have to play it with people that make the game fun.  Playing online doesn't do it.  You can play a serious game of Bridge and love it, but with Tichu, it's best side is the turning of the tide in the middle of a hand and the suspense of calling Tichu.  These don't work in a serious game, but are great in a fun environment.  You really have so little information as to where cards are, it is all about playing the luck and percentages.  Making high percentage plays and playing the hand correctly given the situation of Tichu calls.  I like to give my partners grieve in this game to jazz it up a bit, otherwise it is rather dry and shallow.  Arguing about how to play certain situations is an attribute to this game rather than a detriment.  You have to play it with that in mind.  Otherwise, you will find the game lacking in the depth that you would find from Bridge or Pinochle and you will just be bored.

RATING: 8
Rated 6 after one play. Ouch. That hurt. I played a learning game and tried to survive at the table with three other players. So, it's hard to rate a game realizing that I spent the time just trying to decipher some sort of strategy. 

A "6" reflects my willingness to try the game. But I can already tell this is better than Euchre, Hearts, 500, Spades, etc. The only question is if I'll play this with enough frequency to get to the level where I can enjoy it.

Update: OK... a 2nd play, and I felt a bit more confident at the table. This is the best game I've played with essentially a standard deck of cards. I still struggle to figure out how to balance claiming the lead with having enough gas to finish out, but that will come with experience.

It's tough... if this plays in 20 minutes or so, then I can be convinced to keep trying this. But, if it takes 45 minutes, then I'd rather be playing stuff like Glory to Rome or other "superfillers." I'll continue to try this one, however.

Update: 3rd play, and I'm bumping this up to a 7. OK, I finally feel like I have a sense of how to freakin' play a hand. I'm learning the strategy, and yes, this is the best basic card game I've played. Count me in for being willing to play this with the right crowd.

Update: It took me less than 24 hours to get in my fourth play. I want to keep playing this game. Having played a ton of card games growing up, there's a bit of nostalgia to busting out a good ol' card game. But this is not your grandmother's card game. This has every bit of strategy that fans of the game reference, and I like it more with each play. There is, however, certainly a barrier to entry.

Update: 10+ plays now, and this is my go-to game for people who like traditional card games. I've indoctrinated some coworkers, and this is turning into our lunchtime game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
:indigo: :( :( :( :) Not technically a trick-taking game; apparently it's a climbing game, whatever the hell that is. Tichu is explainable in a few minutes, totally portable, accessible to anyone who's played poker and a game like bridge or spades. There is a non-trivial degree of luck but over a first-to-1000-point game, skill will usually win out. Newcomers are always shy about calling Tichu; they should get over it as this just slows the game down. Tichu is a perfect warm-up or cool-down game for the beginning or end of an evening, but with the right group I could play multiple games back to back.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Considering that I'm a bit biased against card games, this one is one of the best.

I dig the partnership play. I like that there is some intricacy to the scoring and budgeting the use of your hand strength.

What I don't care for about card games is that each hand is essentially played in isolation. Sure, you are playing to a certain point goal that may cause you to take wilder risks if you trail, but its not like a game where you are trying to rev up an economy, convert influence into gold into points...or otherwise build on each turn as you go. So in that sense, I think card games are a limited return for my gaming time.

All that said, Tichu provides some opportunity for creativity, some bluffing (always good), some teamwork, and plays very fluidly. I don't see myself requesting it...but I think I'd be happy to play anytime someone in the group had a hankering.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Best team card game i have played.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The bottom line: this is a really good partnership game and miles beyond the only other climbing game that I've played: Gang of Four. In Tichu the combinations you can play are more rigid (as you have to follow the exact same combination with 5 card plays rather than anything that involves 5 cards), and the deck standard. This makes bombs much less prevalent in this game, but I like the overall system much better in this, particularly because one can make long straights or double/triple pair plays that are almost impossible to follow. The removal of flushes (which are annoyingly common in Gang of Four because of the 3 suited deck) is huge and makes the game much better as well.

The joy in climbing games to me is being able to see the best path to take with your hand, and then following through with it. Tichu also gains points for awarding points to people based on gutsy bidding (calling Tichu) and by helping their partner out when they call it (or hindering the opponent who tries it).

I really like this, and I quickly raised this from an 8 to a 9. This is easily up there with my other favourite card games (Spades, Cosmic Eidex, Doppelkopf) and is among my favourite games period now. It's presently a default game to end sessions with in one of my groups and it has fervent devotees in the other as well. Fantastic stuff.

Aside: Tichu is a 4 player, partnership based game. Though the box says it can scale and rules are included for that no one I know is willing to play the game that way. There are too many games out there that are specifically good for each scale to justify trying to force this one onto the table with anything other than 4.

Aside 2: This is a great cross-over game for people who play stuff like Spades. The rules take a game or two to get a handle on, but this game seems to have legs with almost every card player I know who has tried it. Great potential family and/or game to play while having a few with the guys/girls.

Minor note: When playing with Mary Tomaszewski, be sure to pass her the doge hat wearing, crack smoking dog card, she'll be sure to thank you.

RATING: 2
Given to me as a gift; unfortunately, upon opening I found it is a weird mix of various trick taking games, poker, and additional complexity of pairing up with another player. All elements on their own I can appreciate to more or lesser extents; combined I draw the line. I'm sure it is a good cardgame, but it's just not my cup of tea. I won't play this game unless forced, and will therefore keep the game unrated.

[b]Update[/b] Games have a way of working themselves onto the table. Tichu proved no exception. I recall my previous statement about it being a good cardgame: it is in fact boring and unpredictable as hell, deriving more enjoyment from the weird non-regular gameplay than it does from clever tricks and stunts. Control is limited, so is guesstimating what others hold, there is no clear way to inform your partner what you are up to or are capable of, and the rules contain really weird and counter-intuitive things. My girlfriend, who has lots of experience with traditional partnership cardgames, proclaimed this game silly and irritating. ’Nuff said, really, I shan’t be playing again.

Oh yeah, before I forget: before writing this comment I searched high and low across the Internets to find out if someone was capable of explaining what is so [i]appealing[/i] about this game. Appealing in the sense that it provides an explanation why some people would play it more than 10.000 times. I read reviews, I read strategy guides, I read comments, and did so in multiple languages. Discounting self-referential answers like ‘Because it’s so addictive!’ and general remarks like ‘Because every game is different!’ (well, duh!) [b]none[/b] of these sources could provide me with a straightforward answer. The game either gets to you, or it doesn’t. In my case, it doesn’t.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Tichu is an enjoyable enough partnership card game of the "climbing" ilk. The special cards are the key element of its appeal; they, plus the passing, add some key tension even before hands begin and allow for some interesting tactical swings. (The downside is that they are unintuitive enough to make teaching this game pretty challenging).

That said, the game's flaws really do bug. Passing decisions are usually of more interest than actually playing out a hand, which is fairly rote and mechanical except for when unexpected bombs muck things up. The game also goes out of its way to value good hands over good play; not only are high cards empirically better than low cards, but the bidding system ensures that an opponent's good hand is extra valuable. And finally, the game simply overstays its welcome; it has the heft of a 45-minute game, but stretches out in some cases past the two-hour mark.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Components: Quality cards with simple, functional graphic design. The 5/10/K cards should have an indication that they are worth points. The specialty cards should have an indication of their powers, and their point value.

Experience: Great take on the 'climbing' genre. This is both more complex (because of the special cards and different card combinations) and more chaotic (because of the special cards and the threat of bombs) than the public domain version I first learned. I enjoy the partnership aspect of it.

Each hand is interesting and tense. And unlike in a trick-taking game like Bridge, where if the opposing team makes their contract there isn't as much tension in playing out the hand, in Tichu just because one opponent goes out first doesn't mean I won't restructure my hand to try to defeat the remaining opponent.

First Played: 2005-04-17
Last Played: 2015-12-19
Plays (19):
4 players: 18
5 players: 1
vs. robot: 100 (not included in total)

RATING: 9
[b]What I like:[/b] This partnership-based ladder/trick-taking game is one of the deepest card games I know, bar Bridge.  It encourages repeated plays (ideally with the same partner), and there are many layers to the strategies that I keep finding new things to learn

[b]What I dislike:[/b] the teaching isn't bad, but getting people to the point where they are comfortable playing, is time-consuming.

[b]Colour Blindness:[/b] the symbology on the suits is very good, but there are times that the Phoenix/dragon are confused, esp. if you use different decks.

[b]Other Notes:[/b]
I have three different copies at this time.  German Special edition, Metal tin and Italian version.

RATING: 6
Cerebral trick taking game, played in pairs. A standard deck of cards, plus four special cards (dog, phoenix, dragon, bird) are dealt out. Players play to the current trick, which could be a singleton or a combination of cards ranging from pairs up to straights. Points are scored by taking 5's, 10's, Kings, plus the dragon (+25) and phoenix (-25). Bonus points are awarded for predicting you will go out first, etc. It's more complex than this, with rules regarding the special cards, plus bombs. Well worth playing if you're into card games. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
My wife and I enjoy playing Tichu with both family and friends.  The game is a little challenging to teach because it is unlike the typical card games with which most people are familiar.  The rules are not complex, just very different.

Since no two deals are ever the same, Tichu always stays fresh.  At my present skill level, the game is always giving me new strategies to learn and try out, giving me the feeling that I've gotten just a little better every time I finish a game.

I also like the exciting and dramatic moments that happen in every game.  From Tichu and Grand Tichu calls, to super long straights, to multiple full houses, to the unexpected bomb, the game offers a whole lot of variety and surprises, which makes it really fun.

For these reasons, the game hasn't lost its appeal after many plays (only a handful are logged), and probably never will.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Ridiculously overrated card game. This game only seems a lot more complex and intersting than it is. There are about 10 different leads, and most are unusual and difficult to follow. Chance determines whether you happen to be able to follow an particular lead far more than any amount of skillful play. Similarly, being passed the odd cards to complete a four-of-a-kind is a similarly random.   
    
On the positive side, the four special cards are awesome additions: I would love to see the pheonix/dragon/mah jong/dog cards in other games! And what's absolutely brilliant is the mechanic for divvying up the cards of those who did not play out all their cards. It's too bad that the leads were not as well thought-out as the other, more novel, game mechanics.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1.75
I despise Tichu.  
Don't tend to like traditional card games most of the time anyway, but there are plenty of decent ones I've found something to appreciate in.  This is not one of them.
Tichu is a ladder-style trick-taking game.  Nothing wrong so far.  It opens with a phase where you pass cards to other players, so you have to think carefully about trying not to break up your sets, but also try not to give your opponents something they might really want.  That's ok, but already we're getting into the part of traditional card games I don't like.  This is the card-counting, predicting, move-reading, deduction-y part.  The part where you have to look at your hand and say "OK, I see cards A, B, and D, which means the others have C, E, and F, and this means I have to play like so."  The part where someone else plays a card and you have to deduce "Ah, he led the Hippo.  That must mean he's protecting his crowns."  This sort of strategy completely escapes me for some reason.  I don't think it's bad, but not to my tastes.

But then it gets worse.  Oh, so much worse.  Because there are special cards.  And each one has a special function, and you have to remember what they do.  Ok, so it's some memorization, that's not so bad.  But using them correctly is vital.  And more than special cards, there are special sequences of cards.  These 'bombs' break all the normal rules about what can be played on what and even about when you can play cards.  That's right, this 'simple' trick-taking game has instant-speed nukes!  So then you have to track not only which of your cards are in your normal runs, but which ones are part of a bomb (and there are multiple possible sequences for those).  
And then you try to figure out the scoring, and feel the capillaries in your brain swell.  For you see, you want to take tricks, because they're worth points. But only certain cards are worth points.  So you can win a ton of tricks, but if the cards in them are worthless, you haven't gained anything.  So you want to look out for these cards and collect them.  Except during games played outdoors and days ending in 'y'.  Because what you're [i]really[/i] trying to do is exhaust your hand first.  Those points you get from the cards?  Those are piddly, dirty points; you don't want those.  No, the real points are in calling Tichu! (or if you're really gutsy, Grand Tichu).  So you want to look at your hand and see if you can win tricks in such a sequence that you can be the first to go out and reap the big point reward.

And then, just as I'm about to declare it a hopeless mess, the game goes and commits one more, truly unforgivable sin.  Because Tichu is a partnership game, so you have a teammate.  Now, you've got to pay attention to what your partner is doing, and try and do things like give them good cards, or pass them the lead, and so forth.  But if they make a mistake, you can't do anything about it.  And in a game like this, mistakes are all I can make.
So that's why I will never again play Tichu.  Most games, even if I hate them, I'll play if that's what everyone else wants to do.  But with this one, I'm not only sitting here having a miserable experience, I'm actively harming someone else's enjoyment, and that's unconscionable.

I'm at a loss to explain why this particular title got so popular, and why it's maintained that popularity for so long.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Tichu is a climbing/shedding game where players are trying to optimally figure out the best way to play their hand of cards to get rid of them all. As usual, the winner of a trick will start the next trick and let them try to set the tempo to their advantage. I haven't played many climbing games (Haggis and Frank's Zoo are the only other ones), but this felt very different due to the four special cards. This one certainly feels deeper than Haggis and Frank's Zoo to me, but it also felt like there was a lot more rules overhead. I think I also preferred in Haggis, that everyone is ensured a bomb, if they want to use their cards that way. It could be that I'm not used to this sort of game and it's painting my opinion of it. I should clarify, that it's not that I dislike the game, just that other kinds of games may be to more of my preference. I'm sure I'll try this one again, but the desire is low because 1000 points seems to be too long for the enjoyment I get out of it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
My favorite four player card game. Don't like to play it at all online though. That said it's a great time, I wish I had more people to play with on a regular basis. Getting equal skill level players is important too. 

Update: Tichu is my favorite game. I play almost exclusively online, but this is by necessity, not by desire. The only thing that can occasionally bug me about Tichu is your partner breaking the game. I try to ignore that as much as possible, and it doesn't come up much.  No game gets my heart racing (literally) as much as Tichu does. 

After over 100 games and 1000 hands I've developed some different thoughts of Tichu. First of all, my inability to count cards has really prevented me from stepping up to the next level. I can count A's, Phoenix, and Dragon and have absolutely no ability to count from there out. Maybe in 1000 more hands I'll have a better ability of counting. Strategy wise, I think the most important part of Tichu is understanding when to pass weak cards to your P, versus holding strong cards and hoping for a strong card from your P. Additionally, I feel it's beneficial to think of the game as a risk management affair. Understanding when to attempt breaking Tichus, calling Tichus, and taking the lead are all parts of the game that can benefit from this thought process. 

The most therapeutic part of Tichu for me is the luck of the draw. I'm a very, very competitive person, to the point where it can inhibit my enjoyment of social activities and other relationships. I really like Tichu, but I also struggle with the fact that although I think I'm good at Tichu, I'm not going to win every hand. As a matter of fact I'd be extremely lucky to win 55% of the hands, but I honestly think I should win every hand. Hopefully, by continuing to enjoy Tichu I can resolve some of my competitiveness within a few more games of Tichu. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
[2 plays] It's hard to talk about Tichu without the comparison to Gang of Four, which seems to be constantly thrust upon you.  When I first played Tichu, I was struck by how much less resemblance there was than I'd heard.  I strongly prefer Gang of Four, which I realize puts me in the minority.  Most people seem to think "Gang of Four is Tichu lite"; I think more like "Tichu is Gang of Four made fiddly."  I do actually like Tichu, and I'm sure I could grow to like it even more with the opportunity for frequent play.  The main thing I don't like about it is that I dislike card games where, rather than the highest-rank cards being worth the most points, there is some other arbitrary assignment of point values.

RATING: 7
I've played more games of this than I recorded on BGG and will play on occasion but the bloom is off the rose. My new theory on Tichu is: 40% of your hands are such crap that all you can do is pass your best card to your partner and hope they can do something; 40% are so good the best you can do is not screw it up; and 20% require smart play to be effective. Among equal players and equal card distributions, that 20% is what you're really playing. I dropped the rating as a result. I still think it's a good game but not quite as good as I used to.

RATING: 8
Played the Nanking version.  I generally like trick-taking card games and this is familiar enough.  However the Bomb aspect is still not all that appealing to me as much of the idea behind that favors getting the right cards and not the decision on what to play.  When to play them remains a choice and I reckon that experience shows when the right time comes up, but not having a bomb has nothing to do with experience and all with the luck of the draw.  The scoring is interesting and I would play this again, especially since this is one of the most popular games on BSW.  But given a choice of all trick-taking games, I would pick others before this.  Tarot being the top in that category for me.

The luck in this game will even out before it's end and more often than not the team with the stronger players will be victorious.

RATING: 9
[b] Tıchu [/b] [i]Urs Hostleter 1991[/i] :goldencamel:
There are only four card games in my book: bridge, mus, tichu and tarot. I dont play the latter any more since many years ago. Bridge is my favourite game but it takes years to become good enough to play it in tournaments and is extremelly difficult to find rivals in Spain. Mus, on the other hand, is a funny and fine game that is the best card game with spanish deck. But in this case is difficult to find players out of Spaın. And then I have Tichu, an exciting game of tricks that works flawlessly, perfectly, easy to learn but with a lot of room for tactics, a game that normally the better players win. Portable, thrilling, demandindg while accesible to learn... you cant be wrong with this one...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Components: good quality of the cards
Complex: not hard to learn, hard to master
Weight: light to medium
Players: 4
Length: around 1 hour
Mechanics: trick tracking, partnerships
Decisions: a lot, what to play, should I go for this trick or wait, what my mate needs
Opinion: one of the best card games over there. Trick tracking game where you need to play all of your cards before the others (nothing new here), but the way you do that (lone card, pair, three of a kind, full house, consecutive pairs, stair) and the information you are revealing during the round is amazing.
You are not only playing to rid off of your cards, but to help your mate and mess with opponents, keeping an eye on the information you receive from your mate and... the POINTS!!!! oh, the points. This is something really hard to master, as I always say to people learning the game... forget the points, you will try to learn and manage them once you know well the game.
A game I could play at every hour, and my favorite card game (taking apart the traditional card games like mus)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Update 06/16:

After almost a year playing Skat I can say that it surpasses Tichu in terms of depth and long-term interest. Both games are equally addictive. I haven't had the chance to play Skat in person to offer a fair comparison in that sense, but I can say that is a more tight, less luck-driven game, therefore raking higher on my list.

Both games are my absolute go-to card games though, suitable for different occasions. Tichu is much more accessible than Skat, especially for players with any Poker background, so I suspect it will get many more live plays over the years.

Update 04/13:

- The most addictive card game I've played;
- It only gets better with time;

- Minimal and cheap components;

RATING: 6
Only one play, so it's possible I just haven't seen the light yet. It's awfully long for how much is going on here. You make the decision about whether to call tichu or not and that is the primary source of points. However, this is an all or nothing decision. Compared to something like Bridge or 500 Euchre, this seems simplistic. Then, you have to play out the hand. The decisions now revolve around whether or not to split up groups of cards and in what way. It feels a bit like Rummikub or something. My more favorite card games generally revolve around deciding whether to top what is on the table already, which is much more interesting to me. Granted, there is a bit of this, but I spent more time shuffling cards around in my hand and seeing what my options were. It's not bad, and I'd like to play again, but I don't see this unseating the true trick-taking games as my card games of choice.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[Initial Impression: 7]
Tichu is a strategic partnership and hand-management game for exactly 4 players.  It involves a regular deck of cards plus 4 special cards.  There are many types of hands that you can play (all loosely based off of poker hands), and each player must play the same, but higher, type of hand as was started on that turn.  If unable to do so, a player must pass.  The real meat of the game comes in calling "Tichu" or "Grand Tichu", both of which involve boldly predicting that you will be the first to play out all of your cards.  If successful in exhausting your hand first, big points can be scored.  For people who enjoy trick-taking games (Rook, Spades, Hearts, etc.), Tichu will become a quick and possibly addictive favorite.  Yes, as with any card game, there is a large luck factor in what cards you draw, but there is also some heavy strategy in planning out how to organize those cards into hands and when (or if) to interrupt that plan in response to another's play - all with the goal of best completing your Tichu or stopping another's Tichu call.  There's a lot of tension and a sensation of high stakes - players will sometimes pause for quite a while, and you can practically see the gears turning in their minds as they adjust their suddenly not-so-perfect plan.  It is also a game that rewards experience with the same partner.  As I've gotten better at the game, I've really begun to tire of it and will even refuse games now, but that is primarily because it so frequently hits the table.

[My "Grand Tichu" Success Counter = 2 (of 4)!]

RATING: 8.6
Interesting to play in cooperation with your parner. The rules are quite easy, but the choices you have to make sometimes can be really deep. If you are capable of remembering the cards that went out, you can have high advantage. I usually play this as a warmup or filler game, because its playtime is super low. The four special cards are welcome these  makes strategy possibilities wider. 

Pros / Cons:
-Every game is different, or very rarely the same so its high on replay ability.
-Very good price/value it costs just a few bucks, but you can play it till you die.
-Fast gameplay, with almost no downtime at all.

-The artwork looks awful and the theme feels too dry.
-Only 4 players can play, no option to play at other player count.
-Sometimes the luck of the card dealing can determinate the winner without any chance of stopping that happen.

RATING: 8
[center][size=6][COLOR=#878787]LIFETIME GAME [microbadge=28495]:star:[microbadge=28496] "PLAY FOREVER"[/COLOR][/size][/center]

Is it [i]Whist[/i] on steroids?  [i]Poker[/i] on Ketamine?  [i]UNO[/i] on HGH wrung from the livers of Third World children?

[i]No.[/i]

[i]Tichu[/i] is the stretch Hummer full of coked-out hookers of card games.  It's just plain nuts and makes my scrotum tingle with that "gotta-gamble" feeling.

And the best part is:  YOU CAN PLAY IT WITH A STANDARD DECK.  Just buy two decks, strip the jokers out of one and ditch the other 52, scrawl on them jokers with a Sharpie and you're good to go.  [i]Tichu[/i] anywhere, anytime.  No proprietary deck needed!

PS.  The 6p game blows, and not in the good way.  You don't get enough cards to build anything competitive—and chances are, you'll get a fistful of crap and sit in the stink watching everyone else play.  This really, [i]really[/i] wants to be a 4p-only game.

PPS.  The top end is kind of flat.  There's the initial learning curve of figuring out what the hell "good play" is, then there's figuring out how to pull off a Tichu and a Grand, and then there's mastering good partner play (to go out 1-2).  Beyond that—well, things just kind of flatten out and the decision space you arrive in is a lot smaller than the one you imagined while hacking your way upward to the top of [i]Tichu[/i] Mountain.  It ends up like this:  Once you've mastered all the preceding problems, the game just comes down to 

1) Getting good cards, and 

2) Not blowing it with bone-headed play.  

And that's... just about it.  There's very little you can do with a crap hand; there's really no such thing as clever defensive play with a fistful of junk.  You just get to sit and take it in the face like a pornstar.  I used to rate this game a 10 as I was making my way up...  Now that I've topped out it's really an 8.  It's still a great game—it's just that the luck/skill ratio is out of whack such that skill cannot overcome bad luck.

[microbadge=107]

Here's our copy of the 2004 edition that came with one Tichu deck and one not-Tichu deck:

[size=7]Fig. 1 — [i]Lovingly hand-rubbed with CHEETOS® dust, rage, and tears.[/i][/size]

RATING: 10
My favourite card game. It has it all: thought, fun, tension. There are wild swings of fortune and astonishing comebacks. Don't be put off by the rather tacky proprietary cards that you have to buy in order to play, this really is a good game. Sequences and Steps don't have to be same-suit, so multi-card powerplays are common and hard to follow, with little actual 'climbing' going on. Much of the rest of the game then consists of burning off your single cards with the Dragon and the Phoenix cleverly added to the deck to determine the winners. Beginners tend to be very tentative about betting, so play to 500 for the first few games. You get the occasional 'blowout' game where one team repeatedly calls and makes Tichu, but mostly the distribution of good and bad hands is pretty fair, leading to tight scoring and genuinely thrilling play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
I've only played 1 complete game.  I agree it's good, but I'm not yet enamored like the rest of BGG.

I can't really put my finger on it.  Maybe it was just a little too much to take in for a single play.  I'm more than willing to give it another go.

In the meantime, I've got to rank 99 or Hearts or other card games slightly higher.

May09: Okay, the more I play it the more I like it.  We play it at lunch some now without the 4 special cards.  I actually enjoy it that way even more.  It's focused more on forming your hand into the most efficient melds you can while still breaking it up to steal the lead.

I think the best part is just the sheer number of options you have each turn.  Standard trick taking games are very limited in what you can play.  In Tichu, the sky is the limit most of the time.

I also like the Tichu scoring the best of all the climbing games.  Having the 5-10-K worth points and the last player not getting their cards gives a great incentive structure.  And the "Tichu" wager is just that addicting.

Sep09:  Alright, 30ish play laters I'll bump it to a 10.  I still want to play it and suspect I'll go on wanting to play it.

I'm not saying it's the best card game out there, but I am saying its *among* the best.

RATING: 8.5
A complete rip-off/updating of Gang of Four, Big Two and other Chinese Public domain games going by the dates, unless I am missing something.

Now that that is out of the way and I can't believe no one else mentioned it, this is essentially a partnership version of GOF which adds to the game nicely along with the special cards. There are some other slight differences too and I do miss not being able to play a flush but this is a direct derivative with slight but definite improvements, this coupled with the partnership aspect does give you a little more to think about here but not really deeper per se. 

Things to think about: 

 - 200 point bonus if you and your partner go out first (maybe it should be 100?).

 - Trying to get the 25 point bonus while avoiding the 25 point penalty card

 - When to call a Tichu or Grand Tichu. 

They don't sound like much but there is a lot of skill in all this and they are all improvements over GOF IMO. I also think the partnership aspect helps to mitigate the luck which is a great thing too.

Speaking of Tichu's it feels a little like Spades even in the partnership aspect although it is easier to help your partner or hurt your opponents in that game which is why it is so good. Here the "Tichu" is very much like the "Nil" in Spades and "Grand Tichu" is very much like the "Double Nil" in Spades. You are going out on a limb saying you will go out first upping the stakes, a great option in both games and can skillfully be pulled off with the right hand. 

Certainly addicting and right up there with Spades, Bridge and Schafkopf (Sheepshead) for me as the best meaty card games
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Tichu is a game that will feel strange to most people the first time they play it, but it is not super hard to learn.  Once they play through a few hands they will understand how it works.  The biggest positive here is the fun factor that comes from bombs and the tenseness of trying to go tichu or even grand tichu.

People who enjoy spades should really look into tichu.  This is really a wonderful partnership trick-taking card game.  If you don't have the actual tichu deck don't let that stop you.  Grab four jokers and mark them as the special cards and mix them into a standard 52 card deck.

It's still a card game after all and so sometimes you can just get great hands, but it's fine because you can play a round very quickly and so you just play for a while and it'll even out.

RATING: 7
I keep saying that I'm going to play this.  Then I read the rulebook again, get confused, and put it back in the closet.

How is it that I can grok 18xx and wargame rulebooks, but this one just leaves me lost?

---- 
Having finally played this a couple of times, it turns out that the rules are simple.  The rule book in the Abacus version, however, is god awful. It spends more time on the fake "ancient Chinese secret" aspect of the game than it does on clear instructions of how to play.

The Rio Grande Games version has a much improved rule book.  Also, for reasons unknown, Abacus went super cheap on their set. The 2nd deck of cards is just a regular 52 card deck.  The four special Tichu cards are missing.  Rio Grande supplies two complete decks in their set.

== 
Short version.
The Abacus version is inferior to the Rio Grande version.

RATING: 7
I kept thinking I wanted to get this game, but it's not often that I have exactly 4 at the table who want to play it.  I did finally get it, but my copy doesn't come out that often.  CABS has multiple copies in its library, so when I do play we usually play a library copy.  I could see this being a great game for a regular group of 4 to pull out over and over again.  It lasts longer than you think it will, though, so we have a lot of aborted games.  By the way, if you're thinking of getting it, you absolutely should hold out for the new printing (in 2008) by Rio Grande Games.  It has two complete Tichu decks, whereas the old version has one Tichu deck and one Tichu-suited regular deck.

RATING: 7
Starting player plays a poker hand and other players try to follow with same type, but higher. Four special cards help. Phoenix is wild, dragon beats all, dog jumps turn to partner, and mah-jong starts plus allows you to wish for card. 5s, 10s, and Ks (10) score with -25 for phoenix and 25 for dragon. Note with dragon win hand goes to other team. Call Tichu if think will go out first.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Awesome! This game allows you so much flexibility, each hand is a gem of a game by itself. The impact of a bad draw is considerably lessened by the options you have about how you organize your hand and when you choose to play your cards. The 4 special cards, bombs, calling Tichu -- all add flavor and lead to great variety in gameplay possibilities. You gotta love a game that lets you make a tremendous comeback when you're way behind, too. If it took a little less time to teach to newcomers, I'd insist on rating it 11.

Sad note: I had this as my lone 10 for years, but I have had to knock it down a half-point. This is not an easy game for a newbie to play well. If of your four players, there is one who does not understand that you can't play all your high cards first (and therefore never goes out) or who just never calls Tichu, it really handicaps his team. That might have knocked it down more than 1/2 point, but I love Tichu too much to rate it less than 9.5.

RATING: N/A
Tichu is a partnership climbing card game, and the object of play is to rid yourself of your hand, preferably while scoring points in the process.

The deck is a standard 52-card pack with four special cards added: dog, phoenix, dragon and Mah Jong (1). When it's your turn, you may either beat the current top card combination — single card, pair of cards, sequence of pairs, full house, etc. — or pass. If play passes all the way back to the player who laid the top cards, he wins the trick, clears the cards, and can lead the next one. The card led determines the only combination of cards that can be played on that trick, so if a single card is led, then only single cards are played; if a straight of seven cards is led, then only straights of seven cards can be played, etc.

RATING: 6
Ein Stichspiel, nicht unbedingt MEIN Stichspiel ;).
Das Spiel funktioniert zwingend nur mit 4 Spielern. Alle Karten werden ausgeteilt und die Spieler die sich gegenüber sitzen spielen im Team zusammen.
Bevor man die erste Karte spielt, darf man einen Tichu aussprechen, das heisst man wird als erster alle seine Handkarten los. Wenn man es schafft +100 Punkte fürs Team, andernfalls -100.
Ausgespielt werden dürfen Einzelkarten, (aufeinanderfolgende) Paare, Drillinge, Vierlinge (Stechen alles), Full House, Strassen (min 5 Karten).
Die ausgespielten Karten können nur mit gleichem Typ überboten werden (Paare auf Paare, höherwertiger Drilling kann ich auf kein Paar spielen).
Ziel ist es seine Karten los zu werden, am Ende einer Runde erhält man Punkte für Karten im Stich.
Habs verstanden, das Spiel ist auch ganz gut, aber mir tatsächlich zu hoch. Es ist ein Spiel für welche die es ständig spielen und auch mit einem Blick ihre Handkarten einschätzen können. Das kann ich nicht und habe auch kein Interesse es mir anzueignen...
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Tichu has quite a cult following on this site and after one evening playing a few hands I would like to sign up as well.

I am not very familiar with the wide range of trick taking or partnership games but the multiple choices you have on each turn the role of the special cards and the scoring system have great appeal for me.

The rules seem initially confusing and slightly intimidating but play the first round open and immediately the subtleties, rhythms and originality of this game become apparent.  I now will carry a set everywhere and try and convert as many people as possible.  Join the cult (but dont drink the Kool-Aid)

RATING: 6
Played only on BSW. Fun to play if you have a friend as your partner, that is, someone willing to forgive your mistakes, but not a game I like to play with random people on the 'Net.Took me several plays to get to this level of appreciation, though.  The scoring is pretty inelegant, so the strategy can take a while to grasp.  Also, what you can lead and can't lead is not entirely logical (running pairs is fine, but, for some reason, running triplets isn't).  I'm also not sure that the luck will necessarily even out over the course of a typical 1000 point game.  No worse than Lost Cities in that regard though.

UPDATE: Tichu often bores me now, and the importance of luck is growing increasingly obvious (although being able to assess your hand accurately [i]is[/i] important). I'm not sure that the passing mechanism quite works either. The emperor has no clothes. I'm dropping my rating to a 5, probably with nowhere to go but down.

RATING: 6
I was told that most people either love this game and want to play it nonstop, or hate it. I on the other hand am somewhere in the middle: I enjoy this game but don't think I could play it over and over. I particularly would not want to play this with people who are too competitive, especially since there is card counting involved and you can really screw up your partner or set up your opponents if you don't play well. However, the added stuff to the popular Chinese card game Big Two were quite well received by me: the betting if you can go out first adds another dimension because your partner will have to try to set you up rather than going out themselves, and the special cards add a really interesting twist due to their nature of being really weak (the 1 and the dog can't beat anything, dragon and phoenix takes away points from you), but also very strong (1 can ruin your opponent's sets, dog can set up your teammate, dragon and phoenix can beat almost anything). The scoring does make the game drag, and making the score less would make the betting way too swingy, which is my only negative of this over Big Two.

RATING: 7
Wonderful and addictive partnership game.  I had played Gang of Four before, as my first experience of chain games (in which each player in turn must play something higher in value than the previous play or pass), but came away from it with a bad taste.  However, Tichu has many of the same features but the differences make it a much more satisfying game - partnerships make it a team effort (and one bad hand is not necessarily the end of things for this hand), plus the card passing is not a required 'high card to the winner' mechanic but a selected card to each other player.  The subtleties of the strategy are slow to reveal themselves - I still feel like I could play better.

UPDATE: Having played a few more times, my enthusiasm for the game has waned a little (rating now 7).  At its best, this is a great game, but too many hands come by where one team has superior cards and there is little the disadvantaged team can do except for damage limitation.  When the hands are more evenly matched (with people ready to call Tichu) then this is a much more engrossing battle.

RATING: 10
My favorite card game for 4p.  A wonderful partners climbing game.  Have spent hundreds of hours playing hands, but haven't recorded plays.  Brilliantly strategic, interactive, cut-throat, addictive.  Some luck is involved in the initial deal, but a skillful player will make the best use of every card and mitigate losses due to a poor hand.  Also play the non-partners climbing card games of "Haggis" [for 2-3p] or "Zheng Fen" [2-8p] for a similar experience.

Tichu, first published in 1991, still maintains a solid ranking in the top-100, attesting to its pervasive popularity through generations of gamers.  I hope to pass on a love for for this game to my children.

TTFN

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Partner, trick taking game that has a "climbing" mechanic.  You can play various poker hands from a single card to a full house.  Other plays must play the same type of hand and higher to take the trick.  There are four special cards with special powers otherwise it is a basic card deck.  

If you call "Tichu" before you take a trick and go out first, you get + 100 points.  If not, you lose 100.  

My family loves the game.  Very fun albeit a little complex at first if you are not familiar with climbing games.

IOS
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
7 JUNIO 2005: Comprado en Gamefest y esperando su primer juego.
Aun no jugado, 8,14 dado por mis Geekbuddies
4 de julio de 2005: jugado un par de veces. Excelente, aunque tengo algunas dudas.
SÍNTESIS DEL JUEGO: se juega en parejas y es para 4 personas (hay versiones hasta para 10 personas, aunque la versión de 4 es la mejor).
El primer jugador juega una combinación (carta simple, par, trío, escala, doble par consecutivo, etc) y el de la izquierda la sigue. Quien tiene la msma combinacón pero en cartas superiores se lleva la mano.
La idea es terminar las cartas de la mano (14 c/ jugador). El último pierde sus cartas y las manos ganadas y se las pasa al adversario.
Hay cartas especiales que permiten ganar las manos, a un cierto costo.
El quid del juego es: terminar rápido la mano, mas que ganar puntos y tener el control de la mano siguiente, para lograr el objetivo anterior.
Muy táctico. Por muchos considerado el mejor juego de cartas existente. Y por lo jugado, puede ser.

RATING: 10
Simply a superior "climbing" card game. 
Takes a little while to get all the rules down, but, wow.... truly exciting and fun. 
Without a doubt, my favorite card game. 
I'm hooked!
Update: Well now everyone (9 people) in my family plays Tichu and at least 2 to 3 times a week, there is a game going on at one of our houses. 
Simply printed out one of the player aids from here at BGG so that everyone that plays has a ready reference at their finger tips. 
Introduced it to 4 friends from church and now they all play. 
My wife taught 2 women from the consignment store and now they are regular players. 
At this rate, most of Indiana will be playing Tichu while Euchre becomes the card game of choice from the past.
Fantastic game that has earned my only 10! 
If you like partnership card games and you don't play Tichu, you are just missing out.

RATING: 7
A very interesting trick-taking card game. I like to say that the suits in this card game are different poker combinations (a high card, a pair, two pairs,..) and you have to match them just as you would have to match suits in other trick-taking card games. First few games are very confusing and it take quite some games to get the strategy down. It is played in two pairs and is very competitive. I like competitive games :).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Obviously this is a very good card game based on the reviews on the Geek, but I don't really get excited about team-based card games, and this is no exception.  Personally, I'd rather play Gang of Four for the "screw the other players" aspect of scoring and more enjoyable solo strategies.  However, Tichu does have a lot of very interesting scoring and gameplay mechanics (Dragon is 25 points but gets passed to the other team, Dog passes play to your teammate, Phoenix is a wild card but is worth -25 points, first person out gets last person's points, etc.), which definitely makes it stand out above similar trick-taking games.  With the right group, this game can be fiercely competitive and tons of fun, but with the wrong group, it can be tedious and downright boring.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9.5
This is among only a handful of games that I would give a 9+ rating (the others would be Puerto Rico, RA, and Jambo), this is, to me the ultimate card game.  It has everything...enough skill to make each player feel like they can influence the outcome, enough luck to make each hand unpredictable, the chance of something completely unexpected happening (ie. 1-A, or 2 bombs in the same hand...).  The partnership aspect makes the game, however, by adding a whole new level of strategy.  You can easily kick ass with your own cards and end up with fewer points.  The subtle balance between helping yourself and helping your partner gives this game a re-playability factor much higher than the average card game.  This one is a must have.

I own the iPad application version of [i]Tichu[/i], which is beautifully done and quick and enjoyable to play--no shuffling, no dealing! RATING: 8.8
The best trick-taking game I have ever played. It is amazing how much adding couple of extra cards to a playing card deck does to the game. Tichu also borrows some fun traits from poker games - for example, some calling Grand Tichu makes the round feel completely different.

Tichu also has a lot of strategy going on. You have to keep track of many things and thinking ahead how to play your cards is not an easy task. And when you add your team mate to this, Tichu has a lot to learn for years to come. 

The biggest downside of Tichu is its 4-player requirement. I've not played the 3-player variant but I would assume it is not even not close as fun as the basic 4-player game.

RATING: 1
The 1 rating is for the false provenance and actively harmful rule that you can play bombs whenever you like.

I think the essence of the game merits a higher rating, however. See my rating for Zheng Fen on that regard.

I play Zheng Fen [see pagat.com] with the addition of [ONLY] the partnerships, passing, special cards, straight-flush bomb, and bidding aspects of Tichu. No slapjack bombs.

UPDATE: The more I read the Rules forum threads concerning the bomb rules, the more I dislike the Tichu I never learned. Honestly, they break everything that is the game.

RATING: 10
It's easy to see why this is THE trick-taking/ladder-climbing game that gets praised above all the others. The mechanisms of the game are simple, but there's just enough thrown in to make the strategy of the game deep and rewarding to those who play this a lot. I haven't dabbled into the variants that much, but the base game on its own has a great deal to explore. I feel like this is the perfect weight game for my tastes, as it doesn't feel as convoluted as Haggis, nor is it as light as The Great Dalmuti. In many ways, it feels a lot like Frank's Zoo, but adds in just enough extra things to make it feel like more of a gamer's game. Tichu is definitely a classic, and one that I feel deserves more recognition among modern gamers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Partnership game where one tries to get rid of cards in their hands, leading out poker type hands (singletons, pairs, any length straight, full house, four of a kind, etc. but no flushes). Twist is that it is not the first player out, points are awarded on the tricks taken and whoever is last out must give their tricks to the first one out. Players also pass cards at the beginning as well. Deck is formed by adding 4 special cards to a normal 52 card deck. Before playing a card, players may declare that they will get rid of their cards first (called a "Tichu") to earn more points or lose points if they fail.  Fun game that gets tense when someone calls a Tichu.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
I have no idea why this Climbing game is so popular in USA. It is the variant of Presidential/Asshole or more precisely, Zheng Feng. It feels very old as I played these games when I was 10. What is the big deal about this game?

The game has many small rules and high-luck factor with long playing time. It is ok to play for 2-3 deals but not for 1000 points!

There are much better climbing-like games around, namely, Poison, Too Many Cooks, Attacke and Escalation. 

As for better partner-ship games, we got Goita, Einfach Genial, Lost Cities, and Robot Master.

Play them instead of Tichu!

RATING: 4
Despite its recent development, Tichu is not a modern-style game at all. Tichu is a partnership, trick-taking game, and the fact that not even the cards differ much from regular playing cards may turn off board gamers. Team up with your girlfriend against your parents, who may feel at home with playing cards but like the variation enough to appreciate a new game.

If you are brand new to Tichu, good luck with the rules. Expect your first few rounds to be absolute chaos.

The combination of Tichu requiring exactly 4 players (preferably with equal skill), being not much to look at, and a bitch to explain to newcomers, forced it out of my collection.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Very fun trick-taking card game.  Reminds me much of a game I used to play in college called 'Trapped'.  

Partners sit across the table from each-other and try to take the lead away from their opponents by playing better hands than them.  Over the course of several rounds a winner is determined by scoring 1000 points.

The partner/team aspect in this game is it's most redeeming quality.  There is something glorious about good teamwork that leads to you and your partner going out first and second.  Luck plays a role sometimes in this game, however with good play it usually can be alleviated.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
50+ plays. It’s one of the all-time classic 4 player partnership climbing trick games. The decisions come thick and fast on what cards to pass on, when to play or hold back, when to Tichu (bet that you’ll be first to rid your hand of cards for bonus points) and when not, when to be selfish and when to help your partner. Someone’s always going to go out first, and if they didn’t call Tichu (it must be called before their first card is played, but that could be well into the trick while others are playing theirs out while waiting for the opportunity to pounce), that’s 100 points effectively lost, so there’s always an interesting analysis to perform on your chances. The other aspect that elevates the game is the ever-present fear that a bomb (normally 4 of a kind) is lurking in an opponent’s hand that will ruin your otherwise unbeatable plan to be the next person out. And the fun of holding a bomb! There’s lots to enjoy with each hand.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Partnership trick taking at its finest.  Well, Bridge is perhaps the deeper game.  But I enjoy Tichu more.  Never was a big fan of the dummy hand in Bridge.  But as good as Tichu is, I do find a couple of drawbacks.  One is the scoring.  Because of the negative value cards and getting set on Tichu calls, games can run too long.  In Haggis, for example, all scoring is progressive; if you are set on a bet the points simply go to the opponent.  I also like the bomb system in Haggis far better.  And as interesting as the special cards are, I still find them a bit wonky.  Great game still, though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Playtime: ~30-90 minutes. Averages about 60 minutes.

Comment: This is the best trick taking/ladder climbing game I've played. I have a few complaints about it however. One, the game can go quite long depending on if you call Tichu/Grand Tichu wrong and both sides do that. It can also be over before it even starts. I've had games fly by because of a Grand Tichu call and a few one-two going out. Two, is that it's not the easiest to teach players. It's not hard to pick up if you've played other trick taking games, but man does this get confusing to a new player.

My only other minor complaint about the game is that it requires 4 players. 4 players is a tough spot to occupy because there are a LOT of good games that play perfectly with 4. This game requires it and as such, must fight everything else in that zone. Still, I love this game even though it's riddled with a few minor problems.

Side note: I wonder if we change the Tichu call to 'lose 50 points and give 50 to your opponent, or win and gain 100'. Either way, it's a 100 point swing, but not as brutal as losing a full round of scoring.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
The iPad app turned me into a Tichu addict.

The partnership aspect adds yet another layer and the result is very fun and addictive.

RATING: 7
Fun partnership traditional card game. Doesn't have quite the fun of Bridge for me, but has the benefit of being much more accessible and still deeper than most card games. There's a distinct several-game learning curve, most importantly recognizing the potential point swing at the end of the game when player #4 hands over their tricks to #1, learning how to handle the Phoenix and the Dragon, and figuring out the importance of maintaining control of the bid. I'd be very interested in learning how advanced Tichu players signal information to their partners.

RATING: 10
My all-time favorite card game.  Each new hand is a new series of possibilities, and being dealt an identical hand 2 or 3 times (unless it's packed with bombs) will often lead to 2 or 3 different ways to play it.  Tough, meaningful decisions begin in the pushing phase and continue through each hand.  I love how dynamic and exciting this game is; there's nothing else quite like it out there.

-

A hybrid trick-taking & climbing card game.  While not as challenging and interesting as "Bridge" and lacking the elegant simplicity of "Oh Hell" or "Hearts", it is fun and gripping enough to give it infinite replayability value. Of course, Bridge is the ultimate 4-player trick-taking game and in a league of its own with a long learning curve.
Based on dozens of plays (first time on 03/16/05).

RATING: 3
There are many variations of the public domain game Big Two - this is one of them. Unfortunately the weird complexities of the special cards robs the game of the little strategies available. Gang of Four, Great Delmutti, Franks Zoo, etc are better games.  The absolute worst idea in this game is the Grand Tichu. Basically Grand Tichu has a player bet 20% of all of the points in the game on his next hand before seeing his cards!! What the hell was the designer smoking when he thought this one up?
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[floatleft][ImageID=354872][/floatleft][b][u]Initial Impressions:[/u][/b]
Tichu is an incredible card game. I cannot think of a better partnership game for two versus two players besides maybe Crokinole. When I have a group of four players at game night, the first question I always ask myself is, "Do we play Tichu or something else?" There in lies the rub... Tichu's Achilles's heal is that it is only playable with 4. The other player count variants are not even worth my time.

[floatright][ImageID=711341][/floatright][b][u]What I like:[/u][/b]
Tichu is a game that invokes a wide range of emotions. The emotion of joy is evoked by successfully completing a Grand Tichu call to make a gaming winning come back. Dread is felt when you miss that Tichu call by one card. A level of deviousness is felt when you complete a bomb from cards from the opponent's push cards or when you give the Dog to the opponent who just called Grand Tichu. There are so many things to love about Tichu that comes with the ups and downs from each hand.

Tichu becomes the most rewarding at a high level of play. At that point, the game begins to develop a flow. Partners can begin to synergize with one another with a higher level of game understanding. Partners must work together to achieve a win but at the same time you must balance playing for yourself to strengthen your position.

[b][u]What I dislike:[/u][/b]
I find teaching Tichu to be cumbersome without player aids, especially with non-gamers. Ideally I always try to teach only one new person in a game and I always try to have player aids packed away for emergency Tichu teaching. After people hurdle the hump of learning, they seem to have a hard time wanting to play anything else.

[b][u]Overall:[/u][/b]
I think everyone should have a copy of Tichu in their collection. This truly is THE best 4 player game.

[i]Becky is an a$$hole.[/i]

RATING: 2
Because I was obliged to play Asshole during my lunch break while in high school despite my repeated attempts to introduce my friends to other card games, I can never rate *any* climbing game like Tichu highly.

Tichu does add interesting strategic choices in the game, like the four special cards, bombs, and scoring (as opposed to the upsetting and irritating richer-get-richer mechanism of Asshole). However, it's still a climbing game, and all those games categorically get a maximum rating of 2.

By the way, the art of Tichu is awful.
