RATING: 7
I had really high expectations for Tichu, so I probably have a more negative reaction to it than I would have if I knew nothing about it going into the first game.

Here's what I do like:
:star: I enjoy the hand management aspect and the kinds of decisions that are involved in managing a hand in this way.  Do you try to win a trick now or wait until later when it might be more useful?  Do you break up a three of a kind in order to get in on the tricks?  Those kinds of questions are fun and painful to answer and make for some great gaming experiences.
:star: I enjoy trying to figure out which cards I will get passed and which cards I should get rid of.  It's complicated and kind of wonderful.
:star: I love that it's a partner game.  I love partnership card games.

Here's what I don't like:
:star: Depending on how you play your hand, you can be left  doing nothing for a really long time.  It's boring to pass for much of the game. And if you do well, your reward is to sit around while everyone else plays.  That's less fun than a standard trick taking game where everyone runs out of cards at the same time.
:star: This is a personal reason.  I have a much more difficult time wrapping my mind around Tichu than I do a  trick taking game.  
:star: I don't like that the person who gets the Mah-Jong card can make a wish that will rip the heart out of a huge run you've gotten lucky enough to be dealt and render your hand worthless.  I realize that there is risk management involved in this and that as a new player, I have not figured out strategies to combat this, but as a new player, it makes the game less fun and I don't like that.

Overall, I plan to play more Tichu in hopes that I can some day figure it out.  But I would greatly prefer to play a standard trick taking game especially one that has partners.

RATING: 10
I've always been a huge fan of Euchre and tend to enjoy any partner trick taking games.  Tichu seems like the ultimate combination of strategic depth, good partnering, elements of luck, and best of all, gambling!

It's hard to beat the excitement of a Large Tichu that comes down to the last trick!  My first games were the most enjoyeable as I was figuring out how to appropriately play various situations.  The more I play, the more "obvious" the correct choice of what to play becomes, but it's still got a great feel to it.

This game also tends to be received well by other people that are into card games, but maybe haven't tried out any interesting board games.  Of all the people I've shown this to, it only fell cold with one of them.

RATING: 7
Currently played once and enjoyed it.  It reminded me of a cross between Spades and The Great Dalmuti.  Obviously its more than just that (and it certainly predates them I would guess) but that was the "flavor" I got from playing it.

By the end of our first game I had just started to grasp how the Phoenix and Dragon are useful.  At first I was puzzled by them as they can help your opponents.  What started to dawn on me as you can use them to help yourself *more* than you help your opponents if you can play them at the right time.  The "wish" ability of the Mahjong seems odd - still trying to wrap my head around how that should be used.

I had fun playing it and would certainly like to play again.  It didn't blow me away or anything, however, so for now I will give it a 7.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.94
Having now bought a copy and managed to persuade some people to play I can see what the fuss is about. This is essentially a Chinese version of bridge. Tricks are made in groups (a pair must follow a pair, a full house a full house, and so on) but while taking tricks is important players are also trying to empty their hands. The last player in gives away points to the opponents.
The game is played with a standard deck of playing cards plus four special cards - its about as traditional a card game as you can get.
But where-as Bridge is made interesting (or dull again if you get advanced enough) by layering an extra bidding mechanism on top Tichu is a dynamic and interesting trick-taking/getting out card game. 
The down-side is that this is a partnership game (two teams of two), and like any partnership game therefore just a bit harder to get to the table than games with a variable number of players.

RATING: 10
Growing up in Indiana, I have always been exposed to card games like Euchre, Spades, and Hearts, and I was really getting sick of sitting down to play cards, but now I've played Tichu!

Tichu is a game you just have to play one more time, no matter how many games you've played. Every time I think I have the game down pat, something comes out of the blue and I find myself reevaluating my approach to the game.

This game is, to put it simply, amazing. Even when victory seems imminent, the losing team can come from behind if they play their cards right. This game makes every card important, so any hand can be used to make a serious influence in the sequence of play.

RATING: 6
I don't understand why this game is so popular - it's ok, but it's not really worth spending money on a game that is basically just a traditional card game.  And it really has nothing on its progenitor, Big Two, which is a lighter but much more fun game.  While I understand Tichu is a ladder game, not trick-taking, if you want partnerships, play Bridge.  If you want scoring, play Hearts, where the scoring is intrinsic to the game, as opposed to the pasted-on scoring in Tichu (why do only 5/10/K score - because it's easier to count, although some traditional card games and Dominoes use this scoring as well). And if you want a ladder game, play Big Two.  No scoring, but you're always striving to be King of the Hill each game, works at several player counts, and players can drop in and out.

RATING: 6
It seems to me that this card game is more dependent upon the luck of the cards than I usually like. Quite often the decisions seem to made for you. I like the game play, but the 200 points straight out for coming first and second is a hefty bonus that seems just too generous to me. Calling Tichu is a gutsy gambling mechanism to set apart the men from the boys. There are some nice quirks to the game and it's fun to play, but it's no Skat or Mü. The luck of the cards isn't really offset by a decent points scoring mechanism - instead it's mostly all or nothing. I prefer to play other card games to this one, but I don't mind it.

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Tichu is a climbing card game (in the same family as "President" or "Asshole") descended from Zheng Fen with a few less combinations and different "bombs" but with partnerships, card passing, four special cards, and extra scoring. And, while the card play and scoring of the parent game are sufficiently and tactically interesting in their own right, each of these additions, in their way, serve to increase the number of decision points and, consequently, the feelings of tension you will have throughout the game. Amongst these, however, partnerships are the linch-pin; each of the others ties back to this in some way. 

Partnership play, by its nature, increases poignancy as you and your partner's fate become inextricably bound and you worry from beginning to end whether it will be your decisions that cost you both the hand; or, worse, the game. Almost all of your decisions are tempered by this consideration; not only how this choice will affect yourself, but of how it will affect your partner. And it begins even before all the cards are dealt, when you are offered the opportunity to gamble, on behalf of you and your partner no less, on whether you will be the first player to void their hand (i.e. call "Tichu"). It continues into the decision over which card should be passed to which player and what signal you wish to convey to your partner about the strength or weakness of your hand. Are you positive you want to pass them the Dog and help yourself but also risk destroying what might otherwise have been an overwhelmingly strong Tichu hand for your partner? Perhaps you should keep the Dog and pass that Dragon of yours over to your partner instead? This constant pressure, however, also serves to magnify the burst of relief or joy you will feel at the end of an especially well played hand. The scoring, in particular, is excellently engineered to sustain tension and drive players to shed cards throughout the hand. It is also tied into, and re-enforcing of, the partnership aspect of the game.

Regardless of whether or not Tichu has been called, having one member of your team go out first will always be paramount. The benefits for this include the obvious potential for making (or stopping) a Tichu bid and scoring any point cards collected in the tricks of the player who went out last, but, perhaps most importantly, it also sets your team up for the possibility of going out first and second (or, "getting a Slam"). Because of this threat, after one player has gone out the pressure to shed cards remains wonderfully undiminished as you must now concentrate on going out second to either make or stop a Slam as this can result in a huge two to four (if Grand Tichu was bid) hundred point swing in fortunes for one team or the other. And, finally, while the pressure does ease off when you get down to the last two players, the scoring incentives available for not losing the point cards you've collected in tricks still continue the drive to shed cards until the bitter end. All of these objectives combine to help make even a poor hand feel somehow worthwhile as you can gear your efforts to supporting the team, be it ever so humble or cruel a contribution as to hold the Phoenix in your hand as you deliberately go out last!

And, so, to end this rather lengthy comment, I will say that after over 3 years of collecting and researching games, and after several thousand dollars invested so far, I am surprised yet delighted to find that the game I like most - far more than any other game - is one that can be played with a barely modified standard deck of cards! Heck, I'd be happy if this was the only game I owned - I like it that much. If I had gained nothing else from my time here at BGG, it would have been worth it just to be aware of this game's existence.

[i]May 30, 2009: Got the mythical 14 card straight![/i]

Un excelente juego, y más para mí, jugador de cartas de toda la vida. Lo hemos bautizado como el "mus chino".

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Combines scoring and other elements in Chinese card games with the partnership system of Western card games like Spades and Bridge (mostly Spades). Huge barrier to entry, and the learning curve is kind of flat after you know how to play. This means after you figure out some basic strategy and conventions, the game plays itself, much like most traditional card games.

That's not a bad thing. It's a comfort to master a game so thoroughly that it becomes almost a lifestyle game instead of something you actively try to understand, and it still provides tough decisions on how to play your hand sometimes, and it's definitely not lacking in tension and excitement, but it does lack a wide-eyed wonder which you find in other games when you defeat everybody else with some mind-blowing strategy that you can't even pinpoint how you arrived at or implemented.

This game provides fun and excitement, but it is what it is: better than almost all traditional card games, but still a traditional card game.

RATING: 8.5
Finally played with the right group. Right group = at least one seasoned player. With more pressure, focus and competition my card instincts acquired in long years of Slovenian Tarock immediately woken up and I had a blast. Goes from 6 to 8. 

So yes, before I was comparing it to Haggis and it's pretty much unlike Haggis. Some boxes are ticked by both games, but it's quite different feel. This one is about partnerships - I couldn't tell by playing with newbies, but playing it with this focus showed that's the spirit of it. Similarly to Haggis, there's this back and forth between trying to go for long combos or playing it safe and going step by step. 

Haggis is a tighter game and it has to be (it's head to head 2 player), the objectives there are much clearer and more focused (and the frequent use of bombs there gives a completely different focus). This one is more loose, but if teamwork fills in these gaps, it's much fun. Graphic design is a mess: just gimmick, no help with playing the game. Bah. 

EDIT: up 0.5 points. With the right kind of players (which include me of course) it's a blast.

RATING: 8
The game is another variation of climbing trick taking games (Great Dalmuti, Ass, Gang of Four) albeit with Partnership rules, 4 special cards and a slightly stricter structure of following a hand (You can only follow what was played previously. EG: If someone leads with pairs, you have to play a higher pair).

I think that Aldie/Derk is really trying hard to push this game, resulting in it's popularity.

Update: It's a very intense affair. Sure, the luck of the draw can give a player a strong hand but its knowing how to play out the cards. The high scores come in calling Tichu, Grand Tichu and going out 1-2 with your partner. Hence, the partnership is extremely crucial. 

The 1000 point game can take awhile to finish!

RATING: 10
Tichu is a tremendous trick-taking game for partners. Best. Trick-taking. Game. Ever.

Update: Now that I've had a few years' worth of games under my belt, I can vouch for Tichu's staying power. It has been a consistently fun game. There is a real art to playing with different kinds of partners, to calling Tichu, and to passing. A person can be a good partner player, yet remain poor at passing. A person can pass well, yet remain poor at going out. And even though communication between partners is not allowed, a good player will "read" both her partner and other players in order to win. If you like trick taking games and have not played Tichu, do so.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Really interesting game. Shares many rules with Gang of Four but is plays with partnerships and has more more complex play. It takes longer, but it's much more fun (partnership play, the more interesting passing rule). 

Cards: regular 52-card deck plus...
- Phoenix: Solo = previous card + 0.5 (minimum 1.5). With others = wild card. Cannot be used in bombs. -25 pts.
- Dragon: Must be played solo. Highest single card. Trick given to an opponent if it wins the trick.  25 pts.
- Dog: No value, so must be lead. Lead passes to a partner. If partner out, passes clockwise to next available player. 0 pts.
- Mah-Jong (1): Possessor leads first trick. May use this, but not required. Lowest card in game, can be in a straight. May make a wish when played. 0 pts.

Legal Combinations
- singles, pairs, sequential pairs, triplets, full house, 5+ card straights
- bombs: 4-of-a-kind, 5+ card straight flushes; played ANY time

Scoring
- If team goes out 1st and 2nd, 200 pts.
- Otherwise, last player gives cards in hand to other team, and tricks taken to first player out. Teams score 5pts for all 5s taken, 10pts for all 10s or Kings, +25pts for Dragon, -25pts for Phoenix.
- Tichu (call before your first card play):  +/- 100 pts
- Grand Tichu (call before seeing your 9th card):  +/- 200 pts
- Game ends when a team totals 1000+ pts.

RATING: 7
5 plays. Rating 7:
I played the game during my vacation with my parents and my sister. That is not my regular gaming group so the impression could be a little screwed. My mother, my sister and I all thought it was a fun game and would be happy to play it some more. My father on the other hand despised it and before the last times we played he was harassed by my mother until he caved and agreed to play. In his own words would he rather sleep.
Well, well. My opinion about Tichu is that it is a game that looks easy and simplistic but that it actually have many layers. The best part is that you play the game in pairs, that makes the dynamic in the game great and the game gets much more complex. Second, the four special card (with was a hassle for my parent to grasp) and the bombs create even more tactical depth. Downsides is that it similar to some regular card games I have played, and if you play it to 1000 points, it's rather long. I would love and will try to play it with my regular players and se if the game gets better when my father is not whining all the time...

RATING: 10

Because it lacks the strategic depth of bidding, the game remains quickly accessible, but also suffers slightly from an increase in dependence on luck because the bidding element isn't there to help with mitigation.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Interesting partnership climbing game with some weird additions to (I suppose?) add strategy -- there are many to remember, but the weirdest are that bombs can be played out of turn order; the dog can only be led and passes play to your partner; the dragon is the highest single card, and worth 25 points, but if used to win a trick must be passed to an opponent; the 1 has an odd "wishing" rule; and, the big change, the goal is to go out first in a round, which scores you the last out's cards. If you predicted it ahead of time, you'll also score a big bonus.

I enjoy partnership games and this is a good one. The aim seems to have been to add depth to the card play, and the number of weird additions certainly does add some nuance to the decisions.

I just don't see what makes it so amazing that it's worth playing over, say, Five Hundred. There are a lot of great traditional card games out there.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Rating based on first plays with parents and wife. Took a few rounds to get the hang of the special cards. Will take further plays to work out how best to use these cards. Calling Tichu is where the game gets exciting (just like making small and big bets in Haggis). 

I really wish they had printed the scoring rules for the special cards on the cards themselves (especially dragon and phoenix). This would make it much more accessible and easier to learn/teach.

SOLD - I just play it on Android now.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is my four player card game of choice.  The more I play it, the more I like it.  (And I've played it A LOT.)  
Tichu is a "climbing game" where players try to be the first to play all of their cards (similar to Dalmuti or Gang of Four) but it's far better than any other climbing game out there.  The things that set it apart from other climbing games are the partnership play, the subtleties introduced by the special cards, the scoring, and the ability to moderate luck through "Tichu" calls.  
Should only be played with exactly four players.  (Three is tolerable if you've nothing better to do.)  
Read the Tichu articles on my blog (http://hfog.blogspot.com) if you want to understand why this is so much better than most other card games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

This is by far the best trick-taking game I've ever played. If you don't have exactly four players, then I recommend Wizard.

RATING: 8
Awesome partners card game. Can be played with a normal card deck and using the jokers and rule cards as the special cards needed to play. Can get very deep with strategy and is probably my favorite standard deck card game. I wish more games had partners.

EDIT: I still enjoy it but feel some rules are annoying and random. Things like bombs being able to be played out of turn order seems weird to me. Same with some rules on which way you play (clockwise / counter clockwise depending on who you learn it from). Also, teaching the game and the basic strategies is a huge chore. If it was easier to teach and explain I'd rate it higher. Plus, the fact it really only plays exactly 4 doesn't help. Still an amazing card game but not one I'll play 100% of the time unless are ready.

RATING: 9
 I prefer traditional tricks as opposed to climbing, but the 4 special cards do add something.  A fun race to dump your hand and call your shots.  Its biggest sin is its my third favorite card game with exactly 4 players, I'm almost wishing I'm playing hearts or Barbu when 3 other people want to play a card game.


When I win I can always compliment my own play, and when my team loses its because I got stuck with a bad partner.  Its very good for my self esteem.

The luck in this game is magnified with people with the same skill level.  With 40% of your possible points needed to win being able to be scored in a single round is a bit unbalancing.  Despite the possible hand combinations, a good hand is always a good hand, and a bad hand is always a good hand.  You need big cards for controls.  Other card games I prefer have control over "which way" your hand is good. The trick is to not get too clever and overthink it.  It happens to everyone in this game though. It is still a very good card game however.    

The serious learning of the game can be hashed out in 35-40 plays, but there is always something to learn after that and it is quite enjoyable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a game that everyone seems to know, but I had never played. I wasn't sure how good of a game it was really going to be.

The game is a partnership climbing game where you're trying to go out first. You have to follow the type of thing lead (single, pair, climbing pair, trips, full house, four of a kind, etc.) You can declare you're going to go out first (Tichu or Grand Tichu) which gives you points depending on when you declare it).

But there are a few wonky twists. There's a dragon card which is the highest single card. There's a phoenix card which is a wild (but can't be used in bombs) or 0.5 higher than the card it follows. There's a Dog card which gives the lead to your partner. And there's the wish card which starts the round and lets you force a player to play a number card of your choice if they can, until someone satisfies that wish.

There are also bombs, which can be played at any time, which are a natural 4 of a kind and maybe something else? And the first player out gets all the cards the last player out takes. And the unplayed cards of the last player out go to the other team.

I grew up playing card games, so this was something that I picked up fairly quickly. It scratches the card game itch, but generally I'd rather play board games with gamers.

RATING: 9
Climbing card game best played with 4P in partners. The goal is to get rid of one's cards by laying down allowed combinations (singles, straights, pairs, full houses). Special cards convey unique abilities when played and a card push at the beginning of each round can significantly alter hands, for better or worse. Tichu forces you to make many difficult hand management decisions - do you break-up sets to be able to take the lead or do you pass hoping you can dump a large combination later? Do you take the lead away from your partner? Is it time to play one of the "special cards"? Every round is thus dependent as much on the deal as it is the card play, meaning that tactical flexibility is necessary for success. Absolutely fantastic card game!

RATING: 6
Fun, addicting partnership game- is basically an elaboration of chinese poker, wherein you are trying to get rid of your cards by playing "poker hands" (exluding flush and including some others that don't apply in normal poker). I love playing this one online where there's no shuffling, dealing, or physically having to pass cards involved. I feel that in order to really appreciate the game, you have to have a grasp of its subtleties, so it is difficult to get non-gamers into it (or at least that has been my personal experience).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Partnership climbing card game -- object is rid yourself of your hand. The deck is a standard 52-card pack with four special cards added. When it's your turn, you may either beat the current top card combination or pass. If play passes all the way back to the player who laid the top cards, he wins the trick and can lead the next one. The card led determines the only combination of cards to be played on that trick. So if a single card is led, then only single cards are played. If a straight of seven cards is led, then only straights of seven cards, etc. The last player out gives all the cards he won to the player who exited first, and the last player's unplayed cards are handed to the opposite team. Fives, Tens and Kings are worth points, with each hand worth one hundred points (without bonuses). The first team to 1000 points wins.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Typical deal and draw - all luck. After you play a ton of games of Tichu you figure out the several strategic decisions to make and numerous missteps to avoid in order to better you and your partner's position ... and you also realize none of them mean squat because the outcome of the game always hinges upon one central flaw: if you don't draw anything, you can't do anything. 95% of the time a round/game of Tichu is simply a contest of drawing high cards because without them a player can never have a legitimate shot of going out first, barring god-hands. Get dealt Aces. Get dealt special cards. Get dealt high pairs and long straights. Get dealt bombs. Get lucky, and don't screw up after you've gotten lucky.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.25
The passing of cards and scoring system of Tichu are innovative and give the game a unique twist, differentiating it from other climbing games. The partnership aspect of Tichu also makes the game more interactive.

Having 14 cards does limit the hand types and reduces flexibility somewhat, as does the limited types of combinations.

The artwork of the uplay.it set is gorgeous, though the card stock is on the thin side.

RATING: 9
The pattern setting is very reminiscent of a card game called Capitalism that I used to play, which I didn't really like due to how shallow it was (not to mention it is very difficult to rise in position when you have to give your best cards away).  However, with the inclusion of partnership, the well designed special cards, the differences in scoring, as well as the addition of bomb, certainly made this trick taking game fun and worth playing.  I'm a big card freak and I can't wait to introduce this to my friends, I'm sure this will probably become a staple whenever I can get the chance to play it.
